The problem for the cybertruck is that it's only technically a truck and in reality just a boulevard cruiser for poseurs. There's no utility here, and at least 50% of all trucks are bought for their utility. Try towing something in the cybertruck and... start hoping there's a charging station somewhere close. Try putting something serious into the bed and... go home and get the F150 instead. Want to fit a winch? OK, stop laughing.  E-trucks are a bad idea; even Ford's F150 Lightning, which is infinitely more of a real truck than the poseur joke from Tesla, shows why E-trucks are an intellectual mistake. EVs are fine for suburban & city personal transportation, but for long-distance or hauling duties they're a mistake. One-size-fits-all thinking is always a mistake, as E-airplanes are demonstrating as well. Use the right power train for the right task. So the cybertruck will do well among people with a great deal more money than functioning neurons, it's basically a case study in stupidity and vulgarity. Besides the fact that it was designed by someone with a mental age of 9, with crayons. It is likely to be banned in the UK and many other European countries because of safety concerns. Proportions & safety. There are regulations regarding the front-end dimensions for UK cars. The Road Vehicles (Construction and Use) Regulations 1986 (as amended) set out a number of requirements for the design of vehicle front ends, including: 1) The front end of a vehicle must be designed to protect pedestrians in the event of a collision. This includes the provision of crumple zones and deformable structures. Fails. 2) The front end of a vehicle must not project more than 3.05 metres (10 feet) beyond the front axle. The only thing it seems to pass on. 3) The front end of a vehicle must not be sharp or angular. Fails. 4) The front end of a vehicle must not have any protruding parts that could cause injury to a pedestrian. Fails

To replace the Tesla battery back itself with all modules in it would be around $16,000.  A windshield for a Ford F150 will cost about $700. while the Cybertruck retails for $1,900.  I know it's because of the electronics in the Tesla glass but how do those electronics help my carpenters put more money in my pocket? Now we also have to factor in insurance costs. Insurance companies are steadily raising their rate for EV's because of the ridiculous repair costs. Let's face it, the Tesla truck doesn't seem like it's going to be a long term player in the construction business. More like a novelty item for those that would use it for anything other than your run of the mill pickup.

Having owned two Teslas at this point, I can tell you that their pursuit to make everything as minimalist as possible and put it all on the screen is annoying. For one, we've actually had the screen go out three separate times. The screen just went black while we were driving and it was even a little scary. You can't see your speed, you can't control the windshield wipers if it's raining. Secondly, not everyone owns two Teslas. So when you make the driving experience too different from a standard vehicle, then you can actually make mistakes in the other vehicle forgetting you're not in the Tesla and vice versa. We have a Model 3 and a Prius. Every car we've had has the windshield wipers on the right steering wheel stock. However, Tesla put the single-wipe windshield button on the left stock and the parking break on the right stock. I've had a couple of occasions where I'm backing out of the garage while it's raining and I'll push the right stock to clear the windshield and it engages the parking break while you're rolling. There are other examples of this, but this is the one that comes to mind. Lastly, it's just annoying to have to press two buttons on a busy screen just to open the glove box, or fold the mirrors or turn on windshield wipers. It doesn't improve the driving experience at all. Actually the opposite. I think this is our last Tesla.

That's a dryer (or electric range) plug, not washer. Washers use very little power compared to dryers. It's actually for a high power charger for another electric vehicle. While we're back here, you can't have a functional light (brake, turn signal, headlight) on a moveable part of the vehicle such as a tailgate, door, hatchback, hood or trunk or frunk. That's why there's fewer lights when the brake lights illuminate but there are not just more lumens but more candela. In a nutshell, the lights that get brighter can also be seen from a greater distance as the angle of illumination gets smaller. Reducing the light bar makes the brake lights appear more brake-y..  The truck not only has steer-by-wire but also four wheel steering*, Doug. Remember that craziness? At low speeds the rear wheels turn in the opposite direction, up to 15 degrees as the front wheels so a smaller turning radius. At high speeds they turn in the same direction up to 5 degrees, so when you change lanes it doesn't rotate, it slides across the lane line. The steering also changes with speed, proportional percentage so that you move the wheel less the faster you go and if you try to yank the wheel over at high speed, it gets harder the faster you turn it. Oh and there is no spare tire. The Beast has alconterra (sp?) on the dash. *All 4 wheels turn left and right AND round n round.

There is no mechanical linkage between the steering wheel and the tires...no rack and pinion column...just electronic wiring sending a signal to the steering mechanisms attached at each wheel. In fact, Tesla could technically do away with the steering wheel all together and change it to a motion sensor and steer by waving your hand...but sure some fat cat politician would poo poo the idea.

Ah, the Tesla truck, the pinnacle of vehicular innovation, where you pay a premium to embark on a futuristic journey akin to buying a high-end smartphone but with the features of a flip phone. It's like acquiring a deluxe, overpriced electric paperweight on wheels. You get the unique opportunity to experience all the drawbacks of electric vehicle ownership, combined with the quality you'd expect from a budget ride at best. Basic features? Oh, those are for the weak. Who needs functionality when you can bask in the glow of trendy inadequacy, right?   It's not about the love for trucks or a passion for electric vehicles; it's about being seen in what's perceived as the vehicle of the avant-garde. It's about signaling to the world that you're not just any consumer, but a trendsetting shepherd leading the flock of sheeple, regardless of practicality or functionality.  In essence, buying this overpriced junk is making a declaration. A declaration that you value the image of innovation over actual utility, that you're willing to ignore the glaring deficiencies for the sake of being part of a "movement", one that's less about automotive excellence, and more about flaunting a badge of modernity.  Ultimately, the Tesla truck is a monument to our era's peculiar brand of consumerism, where the promise of tomorrow is sold today at the expense of common sense. It‚Äôs a badge of honor among those who measure progress not by strides in technology, but by how effectively they can be separated from their money in the pursuit of fashion. Welcome to the future, where looking part of the trend is evidently worth every inexplicably spent penny.

That was a good and positive fair review with some elements that were new to me and with some really good exemplifications.  A few misses (or omissions) and clarifications:  * The Cybertruck is made in Texas by a Texan company headquartered in Austin. Probably the most American vehicle (in percentage) of any vehicle sold in the US * The AWD CT acceleration is 4.1 seconds 0-60  * The AWD weighs 6600 pounds * The Cyberbeast weighs 6900 pounds * With the windows open when closing the door, the window will raise slightly to show the CT profile * There is one more level of suspension height (highest): Extraction mode (from the off road menu) * There is another button to open the frunk from the outside in the middle just below it * The rusting is a myth and debunked. The stainless steel is incredibly corrosion resistant and much much stronger than the thin metal skin of legacy trucks. * There are more buttons, P R N D, below the hazard lights button * In the back the middle seat rest folds down to become an arm rest with cup holders * There are 110V in both rows * Concerning pedestrian safety. In a worst case frontal hit with a person the CT is probably safer then legacy trucks as the front is lower and is one long slope. A person has a good chance of sliding over the top * The tail gate is spring loaded and can be closed with one finger * The CT can be used to charge other cars and as a battery for your house * The frunk will actually not cut an arm where you put the t-shirt. There it will sense it. However, near the hinge point the frunk may cut your hand/fingers badly * Front seats are heated and ventilated * You can move the passenger seat forward/back from the rear seats screen

Steer by wire is incredibly dangerous because it prevents the driver from gaining a calibration of the vehicle's steering response.  This is critically important when it comes to emergency maneuvers where there isn't the time to wait for feedback from the vehicle to see if you have turned the wheel far enough or not.  You have to be right on the first go in a one-shot steering input to avoid the obstacle.  Also, all feedback from the wheels is lost to the driver.  A truly terrible vehicle.

There is a 48 volt wire hidden where a light bar would go on the roof and behind the frunk tub and both can be turned on and off in the user interface. There is also a hidden nozzle for the air tank in case it's depleted when it's in for service and the suspension needs to be adjusted. Back in the 1980's Johnny Carson loved his DeLorean car.. until one day on his way to work  the car broke down & the battery was flat. No big deal right? Well unfortunately for Johnny Carson the door locks were electric, he was stuck in the car for a good couple hours! No mobile phones back then, he had to wait for people to realise he was missing & get the word out. These days when car makers do electric door lock.. they have a manual back up for when things go wrong & you don't get stuck in your car..  Well.. Now we have Drive by Wire steering... with NO physical back up! Think about that for a moment.. be it the (over the air) software updates, part failing over time or more extreme weather (which can make electronics go a bit hay wire) In the pursuit to being futuristic.. they never stopped and thought, maybe we shouldn't?  ...or at least add a proper mechanical steering like every other car has had for the past 100 years.. 'cause it works, out of all the accidents that do happen in cars, it's rarely a steering failure.  But I think I know why they did this, or at least one of the reasons.. When it does fail or have an issue.. where else are you going to go to get it repaired but Tesla $$

@18:17 "Cypertruck podestrian issues".... with those sharp edges? It's a weapon.
Oversized battery box
Here's why you don't have turn-by-wire.  If the truck shuts down for any reason, you lose all steering.  Even in today's automobiles, if you lose power, you can still turn the vehicle (albeit it is harder to do).  Luxury vehicles would have already had this feature if they though it was safe.  Let's not even talk about the potential for a malfunction that could cause either no response of unexpected response.  And, before anyone says that you have these in airplanes with "fly-by-wire", keep in mind that planes have at least one to three redundancy systems that can take over if the primary computer that controls fly-by-wire malfunctions, thus reducing the chance that the plane crashes in an open field because the computer freaked out (at least commercial aircraft do).  Does the Cybertruck have a redundancy feature like that?  I hope so, because if it doesn't, that's a potential death trap.  Maybe that's why it doesn't have autopilot yet.
